In general, constrained ERM can be even more efficient than the estimator obtained by the augmention distribution. However, by the third point in the above proposition, in the special case where G𝐺G is the permutation group, we have raDIST=rcERM≪rERMsubscript𝑟aDISTsubscript𝑟cERMmuch-less-thansubscript𝑟ERMr_{\textnormal{aDIST}}={{r}_{\textnormal{cERM}}}\ll{{r}_{\textnormal{ERM}}} when the dimension p𝑝p is large. A direct extension of the above proposition shows that such a phenomenon occurs when G𝐺G is the permutation group on a subset of {1,…,p}1…𝑝\{1,\ldots,p\}. There are several other subgroups of interest of the permutation group, including the group of cyclic permutations and the group that contains the identity and the operation that “flips” or reverses each vector.

We note briefly that the above results apply mutatis mutandis to logistic regression. There, the outcome Y∈{−1,1}𝑌11Y\in\{-1,1\} is binary, and P​(Y=1|X=x)=σ​(x⊤​β)𝑃𝑌conditional1𝑋𝑥𝜎superscript𝑥top𝛽P(Y=1|X=x)=\sigma(x^{\top}\beta), where σ​(z)=1/(1+exp⁡(−x))𝜎𝑧11𝑥\sigma(z)=1/(1+\exp(-x)) is the sigmoid function. The invariance condition reduces to the same as for linear regression. We omit the details.

In this section, we develop extensions of the results in Section 4 without assuming exact invariance. We only require approximate invariance g​X≈dXsubscript𝑑𝑔𝑋𝑋gX\approx_{d}X in an appropriate sense. We start by recalling the notion of distance between probability distributions based on transporting the mass from one distribution to another (see, e.g., Villani 2003 and references therein):

Since we no longer have g​X=dXsubscript𝑑𝑔𝑋𝑋gX=_{d}X, the equation 𝔼​f=𝔼​f¯𝔼𝑓𝔼¯𝑓\mathbb{E}f=\mathbb{E}\bar{f} cannot hold in general. However, we still expect some variance reduction by averaging a function over the orbit. Hence, we should see a bias-variance tradeoff, which is made clear in the following lemma. For symmetric matrices A,B,C𝐴𝐵𝐶A,B,C, we will use the notation A∈[B,C]𝐴𝐵𝐶A\in[B,C] to mean that B⪯A⪯Cprecedes-or-equals𝐵𝐴precedes-or-equals𝐶B\preceq A\preceq C in the Loewner order.

Proof See Appendix B.1.  The above lemma reduces to Lemma 1 under exact invariance. With this lemma at hand, we prove an analog of Proposition 2 below, which characterizes the mean squared error if we think of f𝑓f as a general estimator:

Proof See Appendix B.2.  Compared with exact invariance , apart from the variance reduction term 𝔼X​tr⁡(Covg​θ^​(g​X))subscript𝔼𝑋trsubscriptCov𝑔^𝜃𝑔𝑋\mathbb{E}_{X}\operatorname{tr}(\textnormal{Cov}_{g}\widehat{\theta}(gX)), we have an additional “bias” term ΔΔ\Delta as a result of approximate invariance. This bias has three components in the present form. We need the following to be small: (1) the Wasserstein-111 distance 𝔼g​𝒲1​(θ^​(g​X),θ^​(X))subscript𝔼𝑔subscript𝒲1^𝜃𝑔𝑋^𝜃𝑋\mathbb{E}_{g}\mathcal{W}_{1}(\widehat{\theta}(gX),\widehat{\theta}(X)) (which is small if the invariance is close to being exact), (2) the bias of the original estimator Bias​(θ^​(X))Bias^𝜃𝑋\textnormal{Bias}(\widehat{\theta}(X)) is small (which is a minor requirement, because otherwise there is no point in using this estimator), and (3) the sup-norm ‖θ^‖∞subscriptnorm^𝜃\|\widehat{\theta}\|_{\infty} is small (which is reasonable, because we can always standardize the estimating target θ0subscript𝜃0{\theta_{0}}).

We now extend the results on the behavior of ERM. Recall that θ0subscript𝜃0{\theta_{0}} and θGsubscript𝜃𝐺\theta_{G} are minimizers of the population risks 𝔼​L​(θ,X)𝔼𝐿𝜃𝑋\mathbb{E}L(\theta,X) and 𝔼​L¯​(θ,X)𝔼¯𝐿𝜃𝑋\mathbb{E}\bar{L}(\theta,X), respectively. Meanwhile, θ^nsubscript^𝜃𝑛\widehat{\theta}_{n} and θ^n,Gsubscript^𝜃𝑛𝐺\widehat{\theta}_{n,G} are minimizers of the empirical risks n−1​∑i=1nL​(θ,Xi)superscript𝑛1superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛𝐿𝜃subscript𝑋𝑖n^{-1}\sum_{i=1}^{n}L(\theta,X_{i}) and n−1​∑i=1nL¯​(θ,Xi)superscript𝑛1superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛¯𝐿𝜃subscript𝑋𝑖n^{-1}\sum_{i=1}^{n}\bar{L}(\theta,X_{i}), respectively. Under regularity conditions, it is clear that θ^n,Gsubscript^𝜃𝑛𝐺\widehat{\theta}_{n,G} is an asymptotically unbiased estimator for θGsubscript𝜃𝐺\theta_{G}. However, under approximate invariance, some quantities, for example, θ0subscript𝜃0{\theta_{0}} and θGsubscript𝜃𝐺\theta_{G}, may not coincide exactly, introducing an additional bias. As a result, there is a bias-variance tradeoff, similar to that observed in Proposition 16.

We first illustrate this tradeoff for the generalization error, in an extension of Theorem 3:

Proof See Appendix B.3.  Compared to Theorem 3, we see that there is an additional bias term 2​‖L‖Lip⋅𝔼g​𝒲d​(X,g​X)⋅2subscriptnorm𝐿Lipsubscript𝔼𝑔subscript𝒲𝑑𝑋𝑔𝑋2\|L\|_{\textnormal{Lip}}\cdot\mathbb{E}_{g}\mathcal{W}_{d}(X,gX) due to approximate invariance. The bound Δ≤0Δ0\Delta\leq 0 can be loose, and this variance reduction term can be much smaller than zero. Consequently, as long as 𝔼g​𝒲1​(X,g​X)subscript𝔼𝑔subscript𝒲1𝑋𝑔𝑋\mathbb{E}_{g}\mathcal{W}_{1}(X,gX) is small, the above theorem can potentially yield a tighter bound for the augmented estimator θ^n,Gsubscript^𝜃𝑛𝐺\widehat{\theta}_{n,G}.

We now illustrate the bias-variance tradeoff in the asymptotic regime. The next theorem is analogous to Theorem 6:

Proof See Appendix B.4.  From the above theorem, we see that the the variance reduction is determined by the term𝔼X​Covg​(∇L​(θG,g​X)),subscript𝔼𝑋subscriptCov𝑔∇𝐿subscript𝜃𝐺𝑔𝑋\mathbb{E}_{X}\textnormal{Cov}_{g}(\nabla L(\theta_{G},gX)),whereas we have three extra bias terms on the RHS due to approximate invariance. Hence we can see an efficiency gain by data augmentation.

The result on two-layer nets in Section 5 has three major limitations. First, we only considered quadratic activations, whereas in practice rectified linear units (ReLU) are usually used. Also, the entire result concerns the under-parameterized regime, whereas in practice, neural nets are often over-parameterized. Finally, we assumed that we can optimize the weights of the neural network to consistently estimate the true weights, which can be a hard nonconvex optimization problem with only partially known solutions.

In this section, we present an example that addresses the above three limitations: we consider a ReLU network; we allow the input dimension and the number of neurons to scale with n𝑛n; we use gradient descent for training.

Consider a binary classification problem, where the data points {Xi,Yi}1n⊆𝕊n−1×{±1}superscriptsubscriptsubscript𝑋𝑖subscript𝑌𝑖1𝑛superscript𝕊𝑛1plus-or-minus1\{X_{i},Y_{i}\}_{1}^{n}\subseteq\mathbb{S}^{n-1}\times\{\pm 1\} are sampled i.i.d. from some data distribution. For technical convenience, we assume |G|<∞𝐺|G|<\infty, and g​X∈𝕊d−1𝑔𝑋superscript𝕊𝑑1gX\in\mathbb{S}^{d-1} for all g∈G𝑔𝐺g\in G and almost every X𝑋X from the feature distribution. We again consider a two-layer net f​(x;W,a)=1m​a⊤​σ​(W​x)𝑓𝑥𝑊𝑎1𝑚superscript𝑎top𝜎𝑊𝑥f(x;W,a)=\frac{1}{\sqrt{m}}a^{\top}\sigma(Wx), where W∈ℝm×d,a∈ℝmformulae-sequence𝑊superscriptℝ𝑚𝑑𝑎superscriptℝ𝑚W\in\mathbb{R}^{m\times d},a\in\mathbb{R}^{m} are the weights and σ​(x)=max⁡(x,0)𝜎𝑥𝑥0\sigma(x)=\max(x,0) is the ReLU activation. We consider the following initialization: Wi​j∼𝒩​(0,1),as∼unif​({±1})formulae-sequencesimilar-tosubscript𝑊𝑖𝑗𝒩01similar-tosubscript𝑎𝑠unifplus-or-minus1W_{ij}\sim\mathcal{N}(0,1),a_{s}\sim\textnormal{unif}(\{\pm 1\}). Such a setup is common in recent literature on Neural Tangent Kernel.

In the training process, we fix a𝑎a and only train W𝑊W. We use the logistic loss ℓ​(z)=log⁡(1+e−z)ℓ𝑧1superscript𝑒𝑧\ell(z)=\log(1+e^{-z}). To facilitate the analysis, we impose a margin condition below, similar to that in Ji and Telgarsky (2019).

This says that there is a classifier that can distinguish the (augmented) data with positive margin.

Proof See Appendix B.5.  The proof idea is largely based on results in Ji and Telgarsky (2019). We decompose the overall error into optimization error and generalization error. The optimization error is taken care of by a corollary of Theorem 2.2 in Ji and Telgarsky (2019). The generalization error is dealt with by adapting several arguments in Theorem 3.2 of Ji and Telgarsky (2019) and using some arguments in the proof of Theorem 17.

Again, we see a bias-variance tradeoff in this example due to approximate invariance. We conclude this section by noting that the term ℛnsubscriptℛ𝑛\mathcal{R}_{n} can be further bounded by invoking Rademacher calculus and the Rademacher complexity bound for linear classifiers. We refer readers to Section 3 of Ji and Telgarsky (2019) for details.

In this section, we describe several important problems in the biological and chemical sciences, and how data augmentation may be useful. Cryo-Electron Microscopy (Cryo-EM) is a revolutionary technique in structural biology, allowing us to determine the structure of molecules to an unprecedented resolution (e.g., Frank, 2006). The technique was awarded the Nobel Prize in Chemistry in 2017.

The data generated by Cryo-EM poses significant data analytic (mathematical, statistical, and computational) challenges (Singer, 2018). In particular, the data possesses several invariance properties that can be exploited to improve the accuracy of molecular structure determination. However, exploiting these invariance properties is highly nontrivial, because of the massive volume of the data, and due to the high levels of noise. In particular, exploiting the invariance is an active area of research (Kam, 1980; Frank, 2006; Zhao et al., 2016; Bandeira et al., 2017; Bendory et al., 2018). Classical and recent approaches involve mainly (1) latent variable models for the unknown symmetries, and (2) invariant feature approaches. Here we will explain the problem, and how data augmentation may help.

In the imaging process, many copies of the molecule of interest are frozen in a thin layer of ice, and then 2D images are taken via an electron beam. A 3D molecule is represented by an electron density map ϕ:ℝ3→ℝ:italic-ϕ→superscriptℝ3ℝ\phi:\mathbb{R}^{3}\to\mathbb{R}. Each molecule is randomly rotated, via a rotation that can be represented by a 3D orthogonal rotation matrix Ri∈O​(3)subscript𝑅𝑖𝑂3R_{i}\in O(3). Then we observe the noisy line integralYi=∫zϕ​(Ri​[x,y,z]⊤)​𝑑z+εi.subscript𝑌𝑖subscript𝑧italic-ϕsubscript𝑅𝑖superscript𝑥𝑦𝑧topdifferential-d𝑧subscript𝜀𝑖Y_{i}=\int_{z}\phi(R_{i}[x,y,z]^{\top})dz+\varepsilon_{i}.We observe several iid copies, and the goal is to estimate the density map ϕitalic-ϕ\phi. Clearly the model is invariant under rotations of ϕitalic-ϕ\phi. Existing approaches mainly work by fitting statistical methods for latent variable models, such as the expectation maximization (EM) algorithm. Data augmentation is a different approach, where we add the data transformed according to the symmetries. It is interesting, but beyond our scope, to investigate if this can improve the estimation accuracy. 

Invariant denoising. A related problem is invariant denoising, where we want to denoise images subject to an invariance of their distribution, say according to rotations (see e.g., Vonesch et al., 2015; Zhao et al., 2016, 2018). This area is well studied, and popular approaches rely on invariant features. It is known how to do it for rotations. However, capturing translation-invariance poses complications to the invariant features approach. In principle, data augmentation could be used as a more general approach.

XFEL. Another related technique, X-ray free electron lasers (XFEL), is a rapidly developing and increasingly popular experimental method for understanding the three-dimensional structure of molecules (e.g., Favre-Nicolin et al., 2015; Maia and Hajdu, 2016; Bergmann et al., 2017). Single molecule XFEL imaging collects two-dimensional diffraction patterns of single particles at random orientations. A key advantage is that XFEL uses extremely short femtosecond X-ray pulses, during which the molecule does not change its structure. On the other hand, we only capture one diffraction pattern per particle and the particle orientations are unknown, so it is challenging to reconstruct the 3D structure at a low signal-to-noise ratio. The images obtained are very noisy due to the low number of photons that are typical for single particles (Pande et al., 2015).

A promising approach for 3-D structure reconstruction is Kam’s method (Kam, 1977, 1980; Saldin et al., 2009), which requires estimating the covariance matrix of the noiseless 2-D images. This is extremely difficult due to low photon counts, and motivated prior work to develop improved methods for PCA and covariance estimation such as e𝑒ePCA (Liu et al., 2018), as well as the steerable e𝑒ePCA method Zhao et al. (2018) that builds in invariances. As above, it would be interesting to investigate if we can use data augmentation as another approach for rotation-invariance.

Here we discuss models for spherically invariant data, and how data augmentation may be used. See for instance Fisher et al. (1993) for more general models of spherical data. In the invariant model, the data X∈ℝp𝑋superscriptℝ𝑝X\in\mathbb{R}^{p} is such that X=dO​Xsubscript𝑑𝑋𝑂𝑋X=_{d}OX for any orthogonal matrix O𝑂O. One can see that the Euclidean norms ‖X‖norm𝑋\|X\| are sufficient statistics. There are several problems of interest:•Estimating the radial density. By taking the norms of the data, this reduces to estimating their 1D density.•Estimating the marginal density f𝑓f of a single coordinate. Here it is less obvious how to exploit spherical invariance. However, data augmentation provides an approach.

A naive estimator for the marginal density is any density estimator applied to the first coordinates of the data, X1​(1),…,Xn​(1)subscript𝑋11…subscript𝑋𝑛1X_{1}(1),\ldots,X_{n}(1). Since Xi​(1)∼i​i​dfsubscriptsimilar-to𝑖𝑖𝑑subscript𝑋𝑖1𝑓X_{i}(1)\sim_{iid}f, we can use any estimator, f^​(X)=f^​(X1​(1),…,Xn​(1))^𝑓𝑋^𝑓subscript𝑋11…subscript𝑋𝑛1\widehat{f}(X)=\widehat{f}(X_{1}(1),\ldots,X_{n}(1)) for instance a kernel density estimator. However, this is inefficient, because it does not use information in all coordinates.

In data augmentation we rotate our data uniformly, leading to

f^a​(X)=∫f^​(g​X)​𝑑ℚ​(g)=𝔼O1,…,Op∼O​(p)​f​([O1​X1]​(1),…,[Op​Xp]​(1)).subscript^𝑓𝑎𝑋^𝑓𝑔𝑋differential-dℚ𝑔subscript𝔼similar-tosubscript𝑂1…subscript𝑂𝑝𝑂𝑝𝑓delimited-[]subscript𝑂1subscript𝑋11…delimited-[]subscript𝑂𝑝subscript𝑋𝑝1\widehat{f}_{a}(X)=\int\widehat{f}(gX)d\mathbb{Q}{(g)}=\mathbb{E}_{O_{1},\ldots,O_{p}\sim O(p)}f([O_{1}X_{1}](1),\ldots,[O_{p}X_{p}](1)).

Note that if O∼O​(p)similar-to𝑂𝑂𝑝O\sim O(p), then for any vector x𝑥x, [O​x]​(1)=d‖x‖​Z​(1)/‖Z‖subscript𝑑delimited-[]𝑂𝑥1norm𝑥𝑍1norm𝑍[Ox](1)=_{d}\|x\|Z(1)/\|Z\|, where Z∼𝒩​(0,Ip)similar-to𝑍𝒩0subscript𝐼𝑝Z\sim\mathcal{N}(0,I_{p}). Hence the expectation can be rewritten in terms of Gaussian integrals. It is also possible to write it as a 2-dimensional integral, in terms of Z(1),∥Z(2:p)∥2Z(1),\|Z(2:p)\|^{2}, which have independent normal and Chi-squared distributions. However, in general, it may be hard to compute exactly.

When the density estimator decouples into a sum of terms over the datapoints, then this expression simplifies. This is the case for kernel density estimators: f^​(x)=(n​hp)−1​∑i=1nk​([x−xi​(1)]/h)^𝑓𝑥superscript𝑛superscriptℎ𝑝1superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛𝑘delimited-[]𝑥subscript𝑥𝑖1ℎ\widehat{f}(x)=(nh^{p})^{-1}\sum_{i=1}^{n}k([x-x_{i}(1)]/h). More generally, if f^​(x)=∑i=1nT​(x−xi​(1))^𝑓𝑥superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛𝑇𝑥subscript𝑥𝑖1\widehat{f}(x)=\sum_{i=1}^{n}T(x-x_{i}(1)), then we only need to calculateT~​(x)~𝑇𝑥\displaystyle\tilde{T}(x)=𝔼O​T​(x−[O​y]​(1))absentsubscript𝔼𝑂𝑇𝑥delimited-[]𝑂𝑦1\displaystyle=\mathbb{E}_{O}T\left(x-[Oy](1)\right)=𝔼Z∼𝒩​(0,Ip)​T​(x−‖y‖​Z​(1)‖Z‖).absentsubscript𝔼similar-to𝑍𝒩0subscript𝐼𝑝𝑇𝑥norm𝑦𝑍1norm𝑍\displaystyle=\mathbb{E}_{Z\sim\mathcal{N}(0,I_{p})}T\left(x-\|y\|\frac{Z(1)}{\|Z\|}\right).This is significantly simpler than the previous expression. It can also be viewed as a form of convolution of a kernel with T𝑇T, which is already a kernel typically. Therefore, we have shown how data augmentation can be used to estimate the marginal density of coordinates for spherically uniform data.

Data augmentation may have applications to certain random effect models (Searle et al., 2009). Consider the one-way layout Xi​j=μ+Ai+Bi​jsubscript𝑋𝑖𝑗𝜇subscript𝐴𝑖subscript𝐵𝑖𝑗X_{ij}=\mu+A_{i}+B_{ij}, i=1​…,s𝑖1…𝑠i=1\ldots,s, j=1,…,ni𝑗1…subscript𝑛𝑖j=1,\ldots,n_{i}, where Ai∼𝒩​(0,σA2)similar-tosubscript𝐴𝑖𝒩0superscriptsubscript𝜎𝐴2A_{i}\sim\mathcal{N}(0,\sigma_{A}^{2}), Bi​j∼𝒩​(0,σB2)similar-tosubscript𝐵𝑖𝑗𝒩0superscriptsubscript𝜎𝐵2B_{ij}\sim\mathcal{N}(0,\sigma_{B}^{2}) independently. We want to estimate the global mean μ𝜇\mu and the variance components σA2,σB2superscriptsubscript𝜎𝐴2superscriptsubscript𝜎𝐵2\sigma_{A}^{2},\sigma_{B}^{2}. If the layout is unbalanced, that is the number of replications is not equal, this can be somewhat challenging. Two general approaches for estimation are the restricted maximum likelihood (REML), and minimum norm quadratic estimation (MINQUE) methods.

Here is how one may use data augmentation. Consider a simple estimator of σB2superscriptsubscript𝜎𝐵2\sigma_{B}^{2} such as σ^B2subscriptsuperscript^𝜎2𝐵\widehat{\sigma}^{2}_{B} =s−1​∑i=1nabsentsuperscript𝑠1superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛=s^{-1}\sum_{i=1}^{n} 𝔼​(Xi​1−Xi​2)2/2𝔼superscriptsubscript𝑋𝑖1subscript𝑋𝑖222\mathbb{E}(X_{i1}-X_{i2})^{2}/2 (we assume that ni≥2subscript𝑛𝑖2n_{i}\geq 2 for all i𝑖i). This is a heuristic plug-in estimator, which is convenient to write down and compute in a closed form. It is also unbiased. However, it clearly does not use all samples, and therefore, it should be possible to improve it.

Now let us denote by Xisubscript𝑋𝑖X_{i} the block of i𝑖i-th observations. These have a joint normal distribution Xi∼𝒩​(μ​1ni,σA2​1ni​1ni⊤+σB2​Ini)similar-tosubscript𝑋𝑖𝒩𝜇subscript1subscript𝑛𝑖superscriptsubscript𝜎𝐴2subscript1subscript𝑛𝑖superscriptsubscript1subscript𝑛𝑖topsuperscriptsubscript𝜎𝐵2subscript𝐼subscript𝑛𝑖X_{i}\sim\mathcal{N}(\mu 1_{n_{i}},\sigma_{A}^{2}1_{n_{i}}1_{n_{i}}^{\top}+\sigma_{B}^{2}I_{n_{i}}). The model is invariant under the operationsXi→Oi​Xi,→subscript𝑋𝑖subscript𝑂𝑖subscript𝑋𝑖X_{i}\to O_{i}X_{i},for any orthogonal matrix Oisubscript𝑂𝑖O_{i} of size nisubscript𝑛𝑖n_{i} for which Oi​1ni=1nisubscript𝑂𝑖subscript1subscript𝑛𝑖subscript1subscript𝑛𝑖O_{i}1_{n_{i}}=1_{n_{i}}, i.e., a matrix that has the vector of all ones 1nisubscript1subscript𝑛𝑖1_{n_{i}} as an eigenvector. Let Gisubscript𝐺𝑖G_{i} be the group of such matrices. Then the overall model is invariant under the action of the direct product G1×G2×…×Gssubscript𝐺1subscript𝐺2…subscript𝐺𝑠G_{1}\times G_{2}\times\ldots\times G_{s}. Therefore, any estimator that is not invariant with respect to this group can be improved by data augmentation.

Going back to the estimator σ^B2superscriptsubscript^𝜎𝐵2\widehat{\sigma}_{B}^{2}, to find its augmented version, we need to compute the quantity 𝔼​([O​x]1−[O​x]2)2𝔼superscriptsubscriptdelimited-[]𝑂𝑥1subscriptdelimited-[]𝑂𝑥22\mathbb{E}([Ox]_{1}-[Ox]_{2})^{2}, where x∈ℝk𝑥superscriptℝ𝑘x\in\mathbb{R}^{k} is fixed and O𝑂O is uniformly random from the group of orthogonal matrices such that O​1k=1k𝑂subscript1𝑘subscript1𝑘O1_{k}=1_{k}. We first prove part 1. Let x𝑥x be fixed. Let A={X∈G​x}𝐴𝑋𝐺𝑥A=\{X\in Gx\}. Part 2 follows by law of total expectation along with the above point.

Part 4 follows from Jensen’s inequality.

From our theory we know that the asymptotic covariance matrix of cMLE for estimating parameters θ=(θ1,θ2)𝜃subscript𝜃1subscript𝜃2\theta=(\theta_{1},\theta_{2}) is I−1​I¯​I−1superscript𝐼1¯𝐼superscript𝐼1I^{-1}\bar{I}I^{-1}, while that of aMLE for estimating θ1subscript𝜃1\theta_{1} is I11−1superscriptsubscript𝐼111I_{11}^{-1}. We now prove part 2. Combining the bound for B𝐵B and V𝑉V gives the desired result.

We first prove a useful lemma.

Proof By Kantorovich duality (see, e.g., Villani 2003), for each coordinate, we can choose optimal couplings (Xi∗,Yi∗)∈Π​(μi,νi)subscriptsuperscript𝑋𝑖subscriptsuperscript𝑌𝑖Πsubscript𝜇𝑖subscript𝜈𝑖(X^{*}_{i},Y^{*}_{i})\in\Pi(\mu_{i},\nu_{i}) s.t. 𝒲d​(Xi,Yi)=𝔼​d​(Xi∗,Yi∗)subscript𝒲𝑑subscript𝑋𝑖subscript𝑌𝑖𝔼𝑑superscriptsubscript𝑋𝑖subscriptsuperscript𝑌𝑖\mathcal{W}_{d}(X_{i},Y_{i})=\mathbb{E}d(X_{i}^{*},Y^{*}_{i}). We then conclude that proof by noting that ({Xi∗}1n,{Yi∗}1n)∈Π​(μn,νn)superscriptsubscriptsuperscriptsubscript𝑋𝑖1𝑛superscriptsubscriptsuperscriptsubscript𝑌𝑖1𝑛Πsuperscript𝜇𝑛superscript𝜈𝑛(\{X_{i}^{*}\}_{1}^{n},\{Y_{i}^{*}\}_{1}^{n})\in\Pi(\mu^{n},\nu^{n}).  

Now we are ready to give the proof. We will do the proof for a general metric d𝑑d. The desired result is a special case when d𝑑d is the Euclidean metric.

The results concerning θ^nsubscript^𝜃𝑛\widehat{\theta}_{n} is classical. We present a proof here for completeness. We recall the classical approach of decomposing the generalization error into terms that can be bounded via concentration and Rademacher complexity (Bartlett and Mendelson, 2002; Shalev-Shwartz and Ben-David, The first term (optimization error) is controlled by calculations based on the Neural Tangent Kernel. The second term (generalization error) is controlled via Rademacher complexity.

We first control the first term (optimization error). In fact, everything is set up so that we can directly invoke Theorem 2.2 of Ji and Telgarsky (2019). We note that their result holds for any fixed dataset and there is no independence assumption. This gives the following result:

Proof This is a direct corollary of Theorem 2.2 in Ji and Telgarsky (2019).  Assume the above event happens. Since we’ve chosen k𝑘k to be the best iteration (with the lowest empirical loss) in the first T𝑇T steps. Then with the same probability as above, we have R¯n​(Wk)≤εsubscript¯𝑅𝑛subscript𝑊𝑘𝜀\bar{R}_{n}(W_{k})\leq\varepsilon. Now, let us note that the logistic loss satisfies the following fundamental self-consistency bound: −ℓ′≤ℓsuperscriptℓ′ℓ-\ell^{\prime}\leq\ell. This shows that if the loss is small, then the magnitude of the derivative is also small. Thus on the same event, we have that the term I is also bounded,I≤R¯n​(Wk)≤ε.Isubscript¯𝑅𝑛subscript𝑊𝑘𝜀\textup{I}\leq\bar{R}_{n}(W_{k})\leq\varepsilon.

We then control the second term (generalization error). The calculations below are similar to the proof of Theorem 4.4. Note that under exact invariance, II.1=0II.10\textup{II.1}=0.

The term II.2 is controlled by standard results on Rademacher complexity. Indeed, by the same arguments as in the proof of Theorem 6.4 of the main manuscript, w.p. at least 1−δ1𝛿1-\delta, we haveII.2≤2​ℛ¯n+log⁡2/δ2​n.II.22subscript¯ℛ𝑛2𝛿2𝑛\textup{II.2}\leq 2\bar{\mathcal{R}}_{n}+\sqrt{\frac{\log 2/\delta}{2n}}.Taking a union bound (now w.p. at least 1−5​δ15𝛿1-5\delta), we have proved the generalization error bound.

Finally, we prove the bound on ℛ¯n−ℛnsubscript¯ℛ𝑛subscriptℛ𝑛\bar{\mathcal{R}}_{n}-\mathcal{R}_{n}.Under exact invariance, Jensen’s inequality gives ℛ¯n≤ℛnsubscript¯ℛ𝑛subscriptℛ𝑛\bar{\mathcal{R}}_{n}\leq\mathcal{R}_{n}. However, under approximate invariance, we have an extra bias term.
